We were in the physical temple. And we were underwater.

The shock of the cold, the sudden, brutal weight of the lake pressing in, the panic of breath held—it was all violently, magnificently real. The memory-path had deposited us directly into the central chamber of the sunken temple.

The blue light was gone, replaced by the murky, greenish gloom of deep water. The chamber was vast, a cathedral of stone. The pillars we had seen in the memory were here, but worn smooth, bearded with algae. The beautiful murals were eroded ghosts. And the floor was littered with the remains of the guardians—not bodies, but crystalline skeletons, arranged in concentric circles around a central dais, as if they had sat down to wait for the end together.

On the dais, on a stand of tarnished silver, sat the second stone.

It was a perfect teardrop of blue crystal, pulsing with a soft, internal light. The Heart of the Lament. It looked like a frozen drop of the very memory-sea we had just crossed.

My lungs were already burning. Sam and Kael were beside me, their forms blurred by the silt we’d disturbed. Kael pointed, sharp, urgent gestures: *Go. Get it.*

We kicked forward, moving with the slow, heavy grace of nightmares. The water resisted every movement. The silence was total, broken only by the thump of my own heartbeat in my ears.

We were halfway to the dais when the guardians’ skeletons began to move.

It wasn’t animation. It was intention. The psychic echo we had walked through was still here, imprinted on the place, and it was using the only physical material left. The crystalline bones trembled. They reassembled themselves into approximations of their living forms—skeletons of light and memory, holding spears of solidified water.

They were memories defending their tomb.

One swung a spear at Kael. He twisted in the water, too slow. The watery blade grazed his thigh. A puff of blood, dark as ink, bloomed in the green gloom.

Sam was ahead of me, swimming for the dais. A guardian formed in front of her, a mosaic of bones and blue light. It raised a hand. The water around Sam thickened, became syrup, trapping her in place. She struggled, bubbles streaming from her lips.

I had no weapon. I had the key. I drove forward, not at the guardian, but at the stone. The key was a scalpel. This was surgery.

A guardian formed directly in my path. Through the water-distortion, its face was a skull of crystal, its eyesockets filled with the same star-pit darkness as the Echo in the lake. This was the memory of their end, and it was hungry to share it.

It reached for me.

I did the only thing I could think of. I didn’t fight the memory. I offered one.

I thought of the lake’s surface. Of the reflection. Of the Echo watching. I focused on the hunger in those star-pit eyes, the alien, consuming greed. I took that image, that feeling, and I pushed it at the memory-guardian.

The guardian recoiled. The memory of the Echo was an abomination to it, a perversion of everything it was created to preserve. Its form flickered, destabilized by the cognitive poison.

I shot past it.

My hand closed around the blue stone.

It was cold. Then it was hot. A shockwave of pure, crystalline sorrow blasted out from it, a silent sonar pulse of loss. It hit the memory-guardians and they dissolved, their borrowed bones clattering back to the floor.

It hit Sam and Kael. It hit me.

For a second, we weren’t in a drowned temple. We were in the moment of the Great Lament. We felt the collective decision, the beautiful, terrible sacrifice. The willing dissolution of a people into an idea. The love that demanded oblivion. It was the most profound grief I had ever known, and it was a gift.

Then the stone bonded.

The greenish crystal around my neck flared with emerald light. The blue teardrop in my hand flared with sapphire fire. A circuit completed. A connection snapped into place, deep in my mind, in my bones. They were two halves of a single thought. The Prison and the Lament. The Shield and the Sacrifice.

The temple, its final duty discharged, began to die.

A deep, groaning shudder rolled through the stone around us. A pillar cracked, shearing in slow motion, crashing down in a storm of bubbles and debris. The floor of the chamber split, a jagged black line racing toward the dais.

Kael’s hand closed on my arm. He pointed up. The ceiling of the chamber was vaulted stone. There was no obvious exit.

Sam, freed from the thickened water, swam to a section of the wall. She pointed. There, almost invisible, was a carving of a single guardian looking upward, its hand outstretched. A way out. A memory of escape.

I kicked for it, the two stones now humming against my skin, a dissonant chord of power and pain. I placed my hand where the carving’s hand pointed.

The stone beneath my palm dissolved. Not into rubble, but into water. A vertical shaft, a remembered chimney, opened above us, leading up into darkness.

We went up, kicking desperately, following the shaft of recalled air. The temple groaned its death rattle below us. The water churned with falling stone.

The shaft ended. We burst out of the water, into a narrow, rocky crevice, high on the mountainside. We were above the lake. We gasped, choking on real, cold, pine-scented air. The moon was out.

We hauled ourselves onto a ledge, collapsing, trembling with cold and adrenaline. We had done it. We had the second stone.

A smooth, familiar voice cut through the night.

“Efficient.”

Varek stood at the mouth of the crevice, flanked by two men in tactical gear. He wore a long, dark coat, unruffled. He held a small, elegant neural disruptor, pointed casually at us. He must have followed the energy spike of the stones bonding.

“The temple’s collapse provided a lovely beacon,” he said, taking a step forward. “Thank you for the light show. Now. The stones, if you please. And the key. We have an appointment to keep.”

Kael was on his feet in an instant, placing himself between Varek and us. He was wounded, exhausted, outgunned. It was a futile gesture. It was a soldier’s gesture.

“Sam, Silas,” Kael said, his voice low, final. “Go. The path down the east ridge. Now.”

“Kael, no—” Sam started.

“He’s not buying her,” Kael said, his eyes locked on Varek. “He’s buying time. For something else. The Echo doesn’t want a meal. It wants a door. The stones are the door. Don’t let him have it.”

Varek sighed, a sound of profound boredom. “The noble sacrifice. The oldest memory of all. How tedious.” He raised the disruptor. “Kill the soldier. Take the others.”

His men moved.

Kael moved faster.

He didn’t charge them. He charged the wall of the crevice beside him, where the rock was fractured from the temple’s collapse. He slammed his shoulder into a precariously balanced boulder with all his remaining strength.

The mountain groaned.

The ledge we were on shuddered. Then it dropped, shearing away from the face.

Varek’s men stumbled back. Varek himself took a graceful step back into the safety of the forest path.

Kael looked at me, one last time. There was no farewell in his eyes. Only a mission parameter, met. “Finish it,” he said.

Then the ledge gave way completely.

Sam screamed. I grabbed her, pulling her back as the rock crumbled into the abyss. Kael didn’t cry out. He just fell, swallowed by the cloud of dust and debris, down into the deep, dark chasm where the temple’s bones now lay.

Gone.

Varek was gone too, retreated, his extraction team foiled by a rockslide.

We were alone on a new, precarious lip of stone. The night was silent again. The lake below was a sheet of black glass. The reflection in it was empty. The Echo had gotten what it came for: a front-row seat to a potent, fresh memory of loss.

Sam was sobbing, curled in on herself. I wasn’t crying. I was empty. A vessel filled with two humming stones and a fresh, silent scream.

I pulled her to her feet. She was limp. “We have to go,” I said, my voice someone else’s. “We have eleven hours. We have to finish it.”

She looked at me, her eyes hollow. “He’s gone.”

“I know,” I said. And I did. The memory of his fall was already etching itself into me, a cold, sharp line. “That’s why we can’t stop.”

We picked our way down the east ridge in the dark, two ghosts moving through a world that felt less real than the memory-temple we’d just escaped. The stones against my chest were quiet now, their light dimmed. They were a completed circuit, waiting for a switch to be thrown.

We reached the tree line as the first grey light of dawn smudged the horizon. We had a long journey back to the city. To the source. To the first cut.

I looked back once, at the mountain. Somewhere in its heart, a temple was finally dead. And a soldier was keeping his watch.

The key was cold against my skin. The stones were colder. They weren’t just tools anymore. They were a battery, charged with a Lament and a Prison, and now, with a fresh, sharp bolt of sacrifice.

Varek wanted a transaction. The Echo wanted a door.

I had the key, the lock, and a new, burning memory to feed into the mechanism.

The first memory I ever sold was the taste of my mother’s lipstick. The last memory I would ever give away, I decided then and there in the cold dawn, would be the memory of this moment. The moment I decided to turn the key not to open a door, but to break the lock forever.

I just had to get close enough to the source to do it.

And as we vanished into the trees, I could feel it—the Echo, in every shadow, in every shard of reflected light, watching our progress. Hungry. Patient. It wasn’t following us.

It was waiting for us to deliver its dinner.



---

# Chapter 7

## CHAPTER 7

The safe camp was a rusted-out water-pumping station, buried in a thicket of chokeberry and memory-vine. The vines were the worst. They glowed a soft, sickly blue in the perpetual twilight under the canopy, and if you brushed against them, they’d gift you with a thirty-second echo of some stranger’s picnic, or heartbreak, or dental appointment. The air tasted of damp metal and other people’s nostalgia. It was the kind of place you only found when you were too exhausted to care about ambiance.

Sam got a fire going in an old drum, burning dry sticks she broke with a methodical, hollow violence. The flames were a weak orange against the creeping blue. I sat with my back against a corroded valve wheel, the two stones in my hands.

They were different now. Fused.

Not physically. They were still two separate lumps of impossible geology in my palm. But the space between them was gone. The Lament and the Prison hummed on a single, low frequency, a sound felt in the teeth more than heard. The light they gave off wasn’t the frantic pulse of before, or the angry blaze of the temple. It was a slow, tidal glow, like the breathing of something very old and very deep.

I closed my eyes, my fingers curling around them. The cold was still there, but it was a structured cold, the cold of a cathedral, not of a grave.

The vision didn’t come as a shock. It seeped.

I was standing in a vast, silent space. Not a temple, not a mountain. It was all sound and geometry. Great, golden pendulums swung in perfect, silent opposition. Gears the size of city blocks turned with a patience that felt like contempt. I could see the tracks of planets on a domed ceiling, could feel the subsonic *thrum* of a mainspring that coiled through the bedrock of the world. It was a clock. But not a clock that told time. A clock that *contained* it.

And in the heart of it, suspended in a cage of brass and shadow, was the third stone. It wasn’t humming. It was *ticking*. A precise, metronomic pulse that matched the beat behind my eyes. The Source Stone. The First Cut.

Knowledge arrived, not in words, but in understanding, the way you understand gravity when you trip. The ritual wasn’t about power. It was about alignment. A balance. The Lament held the weight of feeling. The Prison held the weight of silence. The Source held the weight of the cut itself—the original theft, the first separation of memory from soul. To seal the door the Echo wanted open, you didn’t need to shove it closed. You needed to correct the imbalance that had made the door possible in the first place.

You had to give the memory back.

Not a memory. *The* memory. The one that started it.

The vision dissolved, leaving the smell of ozone and old oil in my sinuses. I opened my eyes. Sam was staring at me from across the fire, a strip of dried protein paste forgotten in her hand. My face was wet.

“You saw it,” she said. It wasn’t a question.

“It’s in the Chronometry,” I said, my voice raspy. “The Great Clocktower. In the capital.”

She let out a slow breath, a half-laugh with no humor in it. “Of course it is. Right in the belly of the beast. Varek’s main office is three blocks from there.” She tossed the paste into the fire. It sizzled, releasing a smell like burnt plastic and regret. “What else?”

“It’s not a weapon,” I said, looking at the stones in my hand. Their light pulsed, once, in time with my heartbeat. “It’s a… counterweight. The ritual. It’s a transaction. A reversal.”

Sam was quiet for a long time. The memory-vines pulsed. A flicker of someone’s first snowfall played across the back of my eyelids. “You have to give it the memory it wants,” she finally said. “The one from the First Cut.”

“Yes.”

“But you don’t have that memory. No one does. It’s pre-history. Myth.”

“The stones have it,” I said. “Or… they *are* it. The Lament is the consequence. The Prison is the act. The Source is the moment. Together… they’re the record.” I looked up at her. “I think I’m supposed to be the conduit. To play it back. To complete the circuit.”

“And what happens to the conduit,” she asked, her voice flat, “when a myth passes through it?”

The first memory I ever sold was the taste of my mother’s lipstick. A conduit for a cheap nostalgia-core feed. This would be somewhat different.

“I don’t know,” I said. And it was almost true.

Sam got up, walked around the fire, and sat down beside me. She didn’t touch me. She just sat, her shoulder a few inches from mine, staring into the same patch of dirty concrete I was staring at. The closeness was more intimate than a hug.

“He knew,” she said, her voice low. “Kael. He knew it would be him or me on that ledge. That’s why he went first. He was always first.” She picked up a pebble, turned it over. “He hated owing people. Hated debts. I pulled him out of a bad deal with a syndicate, years back. He said he’d square it. I told him it was squared. He never believed me.”

I saw it again. The perfect, terrible arc of his fall. The silence after. It wasn’t a scream in my head anymore. It was a fossil. A thing with edges, settled into my strata.

“I barely knew him,” I said.

“You knew him for fifteen minutes in a memory-temple,” she agreed. “And he died for you. For the mission. That’s the kind of guy he was. It doesn’t have to make sense to you. It just has to be honored.”

“How?” The word came out cracked.

“By finishing it,” she said, simple as a hammer-strike. “By not letting his be the last sacrifice. By making the bastards who built that temple, who made that Echo, who profit from every scrap of us they mine… by making them pay for it.” She finally looked at me. Her eyes were dry, hard as the stones in my hand. “You’re not empty, Alex. You’re full of him now. And my brother. And every other ghost they’ve made. Use it.”

She stood up, brushing grit from her pants. “Get some sleep. I’ll take first watch. The capital’s two days out, if we move fast and get lucky. We need a plan for the Clocktower.”

She moved to the station’s broken doorway, a silhouette against the lesser dark outside. I stayed by the dying fire, the stones a cold, living weight in my palm.

I dreamed in gears and pendulums.

***

We moved at dawn, through woods that gave way to scrubland, then to the vast, chemical-stained agricultural plains that fed the capital. The air lost the taste of pine and gained the tang of fertilizer and distant smog. We didn’t talk much. The conversation had been had. The path was clear. Talking just used up moisture.

But the stones talked.

Now that they were a pair, their communication was subtler, more insidious. It wasn’t visions, not unless I reached for them. It was a constant, low-grade pressure behind my eyes, a tuning of the world. I’d look at a forgotten doll in a ditch and not just see cracked porcelain, but feel the ghost-echo of the child’s love for it, a sweet, sour ache that lingered in the plastic. I’d see a rusted car on blocks and get a flash of the pride the owner once felt washing it on a Sunday, the smell of wax and wet asphalt. The world was layered in these abandoned feelings, these psychic fingerprints. The stones were making me a sensitive. Turning me into a receiver for every lost signal.

It was exhausting. And it gave me an idea.

We holed up that night in the skeleton of a grain silo. The wind whistled through the corrugated metal like a chorus of dead flutes. I sat with my back to the curved wall, the stones on the ground between my feet.

“Balance,” I said aloud.

Sam, chewing on another paste strip, raised an eyebrow.

“The stones are about balance. Lament and Prison. Input and… containment.” I picked up the Lament. It warmed slightly at my touch. “They receive. They’re sensitive. That’s how the Echo finds us—it’s drawn to the signal they put out, especially when they’re active.”

“So we’re a beacon,” she said.

“We are. But a beacon is just a loud signal. What if we could… mute it? Or change the frequency?” I picked up the Prison stone. It was cooler, more inert. “This one holds. It quiets. What if we use it to shield the other? To wrap the Lament’s signal in a layer of silence?”

She considered it. “You think you can do that?”

“I don’t know *how* to do anything,” I said, frustration edging my voice. “I just… understand what they *are*. I have to try. If we walk into the capital with these things screaming like a siren, every Echo in the city will be on us before we reach the Clocktower.”

I closed my eyes, holding a stone in each hand. I didn’t reach for a vision. I reached for the *texture* of them. The Lament was a vibration, a plucked string. The Prison was a vacuum, a dampening field. I imagined the vibration slowing, its waves becoming tighter, quieter. I imagined the vacuum wrapping around it, not suffocating it, but containing it, folding the sound in on itself until it was a whisper inside a vault.

In my hands, the light died.

Not faded. *Died*. The soft, tidal glow was snuffed out. The hum vanished. They became just stones—cold, heavy, inert. A profound silence bloomed from them, a bubble that seemed to swallow the very whistle of the wind. For the first time since the temple, the pressure behind my eyes eased.

I opened my eyes. Sam was staring, her face pale in the weak light filtering through the silo roof.

“Did you break them?” she whispered.

I concentrated, gently pressing against the Prison stone’s containment. A sliver of the Lament’ light seeped out, a single, dim pulse, then was gone. I released my mental grip. They went dark and silent again.

“No,” I said, a strange, tired triumph in my voice. “I just put them to sleep.”

It was the first tool I’d ever forged from the chaos inside me. It felt like hope, cold and sharp as a scalpel.

***

The capital rose from the plains like a fever dream of chrome and stained concrete. The Spire, Varek’s headquarters, was a needle of black glass piercing the belly of the sky. But it wasn’t the tallest. That honor belonged to the Chronometry—the Great Clocktower.

It was a relic from a more arrogant age, built when the city was the center of something that thought it would last forever. Gargoyles that were half-gear, half-grotesque beast leaned from its soot-stained flanks. Four colossal faces, each looking to a cardinal direction, told not just the time, but the astrological season, the tidal coefficients for a sea that had been paved over centuries ago, and the phases of a moon obscured by light pollution. It was a monument to a system of order that had collapsed under the weight of its own complexity. Now it was a tourist trap, a haunted house, a symbol on municipal letterhead. The perfect hiding place. No one looks for a myth in a gift shop.

Getting in was Sam’s department.

“Security is municipal-grade,” she said, as we lurked in a service alley behind a kebab shop that smelled of roasting mystery meat and despair. She’d acquired a battered tablet from a source she didn’t name and was scrolling through schematics. “Motion sensors on the non-public floors, camera loops that are older than I am. The real issue is the Keepers.”

“Keepers?”

“Cultists,” she said, with a dismissive snort. “History buffs who think the Chronometry is a sacred text. They volunteer as night watchmen, guides. They live in the upper floors. They know every creak of the place. They’re the real alarm system.”

I looked up at the Tower, blotting out a swath of the bruised evening sky. “Can we go through them?”

“No,” she said, too quickly. Then she sighed. “Maybe. They’re not fighters. They’re… true believers. In the machine. In the order of it. If we can frame this as a… a ritual maintenance. A correction to the mechanism. They might help. Or they might throw us down the mainspring shaft.”

“It’s a better chance than shooting our way past Varek’s private security at the Spire,” I said.

She nodded, reluctantly. “There’s a service entrance. For the Keepers. It’s coded, but the code’s probably in one of these maintenance logs from twenty years ago. If we’re lucky, they never changed it.”

We were not lucky.

The code from the 20-year-old log didn’t work. The keypad beeped a rude, red denial. I stood in the damp alley, the silent stones a dead weight in the inner pocket of my coat, feeling the vast, stupid bulk of the Tower above us. The Clock was a presence, a giant breathing in its sleep. I could feel the distant *thrum* of it in the pavement.

Sam cursed, low and inventive. “Plan B. We go over the roof of the adjacent hab-block, try for a window on the third sub-bell level. The schematics show a possible gap in the sensor grid there.”

“Wait,” I said.

The pressure was back behind my eyes, a faint, insistent tap-tap-tap. Not from the stones. They were silent. This was coming from the Tower itself. From the memory of the mechanism. I placed my palm flat against the cold steel of the service door. Under the grease and urban grime, I could feel the ghost of the metal being forged, the intent of the architect, the thousand thousand hands that had polished this entryway over the centuries. And beneath that, like a single, clear note in a cacophony, was the memory of the code.

It wasn’t a number. It was a pattern. A rhythm. The first three notes of a lullaby that had been popular when the Tower was young. A foreman had hummed it as he set the lock. A sentimental choice, fossilized in brass and electricity.

My fingers moved on the keypad, not thinking, just tracing the rhythm. Tap-tap… tap-tap-tap… tap.

The light winked green. The door gave a soft, hydraulic sigh and slid open an inch, revealing darkness and the smell of machine oil and old paper.

Sam stared at me. “How?”

“The Tower remembers,” I said, and the truth of it was a chill down my spine. I wasn’t just a receiver for lost feelings anymore. I was starting to hear the memories of things. Of places. Of stone and steel.

We slipped inside.

***

The interior of the Chronometry was a cathedral to entropy. We climbed spiral staircases of wrought iron that spiraled into gloom. We passed through halls of silent, frozen clockwork—enormous escapements caught mid-tick, driveshafts like petrified trees. The air was still and thick with the smell of ozone from old electrical conduits and the vanilla-tinged decay of aging paper. Our footsteps were swallowed by the immense, listening silence.

We saw no Keepers. We heard only the distant, regular *THOOM* of the great pendulum, far below, marking seconds that felt like heartbeats.

According to Sam’s schematics, the heart of the clockwork—the chamber that housed the primary gear train and, if my vision was correct, the Source Stone—was at the center of the Tower, accessible only by a narrow maintenance bridge that spanned a sheer drop to the pendulum’s chamber.

We found the bridge. It was a spidery lattice of walkways, suspended in a cylindrical void. In the center of the void, illuminated by a single, dusty shaft of moonlight from a high window, was the mechanism.

It was more beautiful than the vision. More terrifying. Gears interlocked in a dance of sublime, pointless complexity. The mainspring was a coiled mountain of gleaming alloy. And in the very center, suspended in a cage of articulated brass rods that seemed to cradle it like a jewel, was the Source Stone.

It was smaller than the others. Paler. A milky white, like a blind eye. And it ticked. A sound so precise it seemed to cut the air into neat slices. *Tick. Tick. Tick.*

We were halfway across the bridge when the lights came on.

Not electric lights. Lanterns, held aloft by figures in dark brown robes. They emerged from shadowed archways on the far side of the chamber, a dozen of them, silent as the gears. The Keepers.

An old woman stepped to the front. Her face was a roadmap of wrinkles, her eyes the clear, sharp blue of a gas flame. She held no lantern. She didn’t need to.

“The mechanism has been waiting,” she said. Her voice was the sound of parchment rubbing together. “It dreamed of your arrival. A dissonance. A correction.”

Sam had her hand inside her coat, on the grip of her weapon. I put a hand on her arm, gently.

“We’re here to perform the ritual,” I said, my voice echoing in the vast space. “To restore the balance.”

The old woman’s eyes fixed on me. I felt them like a physical touch, probing the place where the stones lay silent against my chest. “You carry the counterweights,” she said. “The Lament. The Prison. But you do not understand the transaction.”

“I understand I have to give the memory back,” I said.

A faint smile touched her thin lips. “You are the memory, child. You are the vessel the First Cut created. All of you who trade in what you are. You carry the wound. The ritual does not *use* the memory. It *heals* it. In you. Through you.” She gestured to the ticking stone. “The Source is not a tool. It is the surgeon. We are the nurses. And you…” Her blue eyes held mine. “You are the patient.”

The truth of it landed in my gut, cold and heavy. This wasn’t about saving the world. It was about suturing a cut that ran through every memory dealer, every ghost-eater, every hollow person like me. It was personal. Deeply, terrifyingly personal.

“What happens,” I asked, my mouth dry, “if the surgery fails?”

“The mechanism rejects the faulty part,” she said simply. “The Echo, which is the infection, will be drawn to the site of the operation. It will consume the offered memory—you—and in doing so, will gorge itself on the very wound it needs to exist. It will become stable. Permanent. The door will be not just open, but built into the foundation of things.”

“And if it works?” Sam’s voice was tight.

“The wound closes. The First Cut is undone, in a single point. In him. The Echo, denied its sustenance at the source, will unravel. A chain reaction. The door will cease to have ever been.” The old woman tilted her head. “The mechanism is ready. Are you?”

I looked at the ticking stone. I thought of the taste of wax and chemical cherry. I thought of Kael’s fall, a silent fossil in my mind. I thought of the hollow place where my brother’s laugh used to be. I was a museum of losses, a catalog of sold sensations. I was the perfect patient.

I took a step forward onto the bridge. “Let’s operate.”

The Keepers moved as one, not to stop me, but to take positions around the chamber, their lanterns held high. The old woman remained, a sentinel at the entrance to the heart.

Sam grabbed my wrist. Her grip was iron. “Alex.”

“It’s okay,” I said, and it was a lie we both accepted. “This is how we finish it.”

I walked to the end of the bridge, to the platform at the center of the world. The ticking of the Source Stone was inside my skull now, matching my pulse, then slowing it, forcing it into sync. The cage of brass rods unfolded with a series of soft, oily clicks, retracting to leave the stone exposed, hovering in the air at the center of the gear train.

I took the Lament and the Prison from my pocket. They were no longer silent. In the presence of the Source, they woke. Light blazed from them—one a deep, sorrowful blue, the other a stern, unforgiving grey. The humming returned, a chord that vibrated in my bones.

The old woman’s voice rang out, a chant in a language of cogs and tension. “Place the counterweights. Complete the circuit. Offer the wound.”

My hands moved without my command. I reached out, placing the Lament stone into a depression on one side of the hovering Source. I placed the Prison into a matching depression on the other side.

They clicked into place.

A light, pure and white and silent, erupted from the Source Stone. It didn’t illuminate the chamber. It *replaced* it. The gears, the Keepers, Sam’s terrified face, the very Tower—all of it dissolved into the white.

And then the memories came.

Not as fragments. Not as loops. As a torrent. A flood.

The first memory I ever sold was the taste of my mother’s lipstick. I felt it again, now, but I also felt *her* memory of buying it, the hope it represented, the weariness under her eyes as she applied it. I felt the memory of the man who mined the pigment, the woman who mixed the wax. I felt every memory I had ever sold, every scrap of myself I’d bartered, but I felt them whole, connected to their roots, to the lives they’d been torn from. My brother’s laugh, not as a isolated sound, but as part of a summer afternoon, a joke I’d forgotten, the feel of grass under our backs.

It was an agony of reconnection. It was like having severed limbs sewn back on, the nerves screaming to life.

And beneath it all, older than language, was the First Cut.

It was not a violent act. It was a sad one. A choice made from loneliness. A being, vast and old and alone, reaching into its own endless experience and *taking* a piece out, to hold, to look at, to make the loneliness feel like something else. The first theft. The first souvenir. The original sin of nostalgia.

I was that cut. My hollow places were its shape.

The white light focused, became a scalpel. It poised at the center of me, where the hollow was deepest.

The old woman’s voice whispered in the void. *“Now. Give it back.”*

I didn’t know how. I just knew I had to stop clinging. To the loss, to the hollow, to the very identity the wound had given me. Who was I without my emptiness? I had no idea. It was the only self I’d ever known.

I let go.

The light pierced me. It did not hurt. It *unmade*.

And in the space where the hollow had been, where the First Cut had been, I felt something else. A presence, vast and cold and hungry, drawn to the scent of the surgery.

The Echo was here. It had been waiting in the wings of the mechanism. It flowed into the white space, a stain of anti-light, a perfect, silent vacuum of self. It saw the offered memory—the raw, healed wound of the First Cut—and it moved to consume. To make this reconciliation its own, to turn healing into a permanent disease.

This was the trap. Not of the Keepers, but of the ritual itself. The patient was always vulnerable. The infection always arrives at the wound.

The Echo enveloped the point of light. The white began to dim, to be leeched of its meaning, turned into mere data, a resource.

I had no weapons. I had no stones. I had only what had just been given back to me.

I had a mother’s hope. I had a brother’s joke in the grass. I had the taste of lipstick, not as a sold fragment, but as a moment in a story that was still mine.

I had a memory of a soldier, falling silently through temple air, to save a ghost he didn’t know.

I gathered them. Not as shields. As anchors. As weights.

I threw them, not at the Echo, but into the mechanism of the ritual itself. Into the balance.

The Lament stone flared, blue as a forgotten sea. It held the weight of Kael’s sacrifice, of every goodbye. The Prison stone glowed, grey as a cell wall. It held the weight of my brother’s absence, of every silence. They were counterweights. And I had just loaded the scale.

The Echo, feeding on the healing, suddenly strained against a new tension. The balance was shifting. The door it wanted to build wasn’t just open; it was being *measured*.

The white light pulsed, fighting the draining dark. The ticking of the Source Stone became erratic, a frantic stutter.

*Tick-tick-tick-TICK—*

On the platform, in the real world I could no longer see, Sam was shouting. The Keepers were chanting, their voices rising to a fever pitch. The gears of the Great Clocktower began to grind, to shriek in protest, metal straining against a force it was never meant to contain.

The ritual was tearing itself apart.

And I was still at the center of it, the patient on the table, feeling the stitches burst.



---

# Chapter 8

## CHAPTER 8

The city didn’t have a name. It was just the Capital, a place that had eaten the villages around it until its borders were a geography of digested brick and rusted rail. From the ridge, it looked like a machine that had run too hot and seized, all jagged spires and choked arteries of traffic, steam rising from its vents in a perpetual, sooty sigh. The air here tasted of coal-dust and fried dough, a cheap, greasy substitute for real food. It was the kind of place that sold memories of sunlight by the hour.

Sam spat into the dust beside the road. “Charming.”

“It’s a front,” I said, my voice flat. “The bigger the noise, the easier it is to hide a whisper.”

We’d hitched a ride on a produce hauler, buried under a cargo of genetically-scented peaches that smelled like a child’s drawing of fruit. The driver, a man with a face like a clenched fist, hadn’t asked questions. Questions were a tax in the Capital, and nobody here liked to pay. We slipped off the back of his truck as it slowed for the outer checkpoint, melting into the river of bodies flowing through the pedestrian gate. The guards were bored, their eyes glazed, scanning for contraband weapons or unlicensed emotion-modulators. Two more tired faces in work-stained coats didn’t even register.

Inside, the noise was a physical thing. It wasn’t just sound; it was a pressure, a compound of shouting vendors, pneumatic drills, the thrum of anti-grav transports, and the tinny, desperate music bleeding from a hundred open doorways. Advertisements shimmered on every vertical surface, offering memory-enhancements, dream-editing, sensory overlays to make your gruel taste like steak. The nostalgia-core crowd had gone mainstream here, commodified and sanitized. You could buy a pre-packaged ‘Maternal Kiss’ experience for twelve credits. No waxy cherry, no warmth. Just the data points.

I felt the ghost of the extraction chair at the base of my skull. A clean, clinical humiliation. This whole city was built on that same principle.

“Don’t look at them,” Sam muttered, steering me by the elbow through the crush. He’d acquired a cap from somewhere, pulled low over his eyes. “You’re staring. You look like a mark.”

He was right. I was looking for Varek’s agents, expecting black coats, sharp eyes. But that was provincial thinking. Here, they’d be better. They’d be the man selling synth-apples with a too-easy smile. The woman adjusting a holosign with perfectly calm hands. They’d be part of the noise. The trap wasn’t in the silence here; it was in the perfect, overwhelming din.

We moved like debris in the current, letting it carry us deeper into the city’s heart. The Great Clocktower wasn’t a secret. It was the city’s logo, stamped on every official document, its image flickering on the public news feeds every hour on the hour. A monolithic spike of black iron and tarnished bronze, it rose from the central plaza, a fist of arrogant time punching up through the smog. Its face was a complex nightmare of gears and shifting numerals, displaying not just hours, but market indices, atmospheric particulate counts, and the approved emotional-index for the day. It didn’t tell time. It administered it.

The plaza around it was a wide, flagstoned pit, a respite from the claustrophobic streets. People milled, necks craned, taking in the sanctioned spectacle. Tourists bought cheap holograms of the tower at dawn. City workers ate lunch on the steps. It was all so blatant, so obvious. A front so good it didn’t even look like one.

“A secret society,” Sam whispered, his lips barely moving. “In there. You sure?”

“The Lament stone showed me a key,” I said. “The key was a ticking. This is the only clock that matters.”

We circled the plaza, a slow, casual orbit. I counted the visible doors: a main public entrance for tours, a service bay for the clockwork mechanics, several administrative portals. All watched by subtle, glossy lenses. Infiltration here wasn’t about picking locks. It was about understanding the rhythm of the machine, finding the off-beat.

I found it in the garbage.

A service chute, discreetly grated, near the tower’s north face. Every thirty-seven minutes exactly, a low hum emanated from within, followed by the faint, echoing clatter of compacted waste being shunted to underground processing. A maintenance cycle. A piece of the machine’s internal logic. In the two minutes after the hum faded, the security feed on the nearest lens, I noted, flickered through a diagnostic cycle. A blind spot in the perception of a thing that saw everything.

“There,” I said to Sam.

He saw it, gave a tight nod. We waited. A group of schoolchildren surged past, shepherded by a harried drone. We used their chaos. At the thirty-seven minute mark, as the hum died and the lens glazed over with a milky diagnostic sheen, Sam was at the grate. He didn’t force it; his fingers traced the edges, found a pressure point, and the whole panel slid sideways with a well-oiled sigh. Not a breach. An invitation for those who knew how to listen to the rhythm.

The chute was dark, smelling of ozone, old metal, and the faint, sweet-rot scent of discarded organic matter. We dropped down onto a soft mound of what felt like shredded paper. A utility corridor, lit by the cold blue of bioluminescent strips, stretched in both directions. The air was quieter here, the city’s scream reduced to a dull, metallic throbbing in the walls. The sound of the tower’s heart.

We followed the deeper tick.

It wasn’t the loud, showy tick of the public face. This was a subcutaneous sound, a steady, relentless *tock-tock-tock* that came up through the floor plates and vibrated in your teeth. It was the source of all the other time in the city. We moved like mice in a colossal wristwatch, past rooms where giant, polished gears meshed with silent, oiled perfection, past banks of spinning brass cylinders etched with celestial maps. This was the truth behind the front: not a bureaucracy, but a temple to a mechanical god.

We found the secret society because they found us.

A door, seamless in the wall, hissed open just as we passed it. No one emerged. Just a rectangle of warmer, golden light. An invitation.

The room beyond was a library, but not of books. Of memories. Crystalline data-slivers, each the size of a finger, were housed in individual niches that covered the walls from floor to a domed ceiling high above. A soft, amber light glowed from within each niche, pulsing faintly, like slow heartbeats. The air was hushed, reverent, and smelled of ozone and aged paper. In the center of the room, standing beside a reading podium of dark wood, was a woman.

She was older, her hair a sweep of silver pulled into a severe knot. She wore simple grey trousers and a tunic, but the fabric was fine, the cut precise. Her eyes were the colour of the clockwork outside, a sharp, assessing bronze. She watched us enter, her hands resting lightly on the podium. On it sat a single, uncut stone. It was clear, like quartz, but within it, tiny, intricate gears of light turned in impossible, perpetual motion.

“The patient arrives,” she said. Her voice was dry, cool, devoid of the city’s greasy warmth. “And the soldier. I am Elara.”

Sam’s posture shifted, ready. I put a hand on his arm.

“You were expecting us,” I said.

“The Great Clock measures many things,” Elara replied. “Not just hours. It measures deviations. Ripples in the predictable flow. You two have been a sustained anomaly for weeks. A persistent arrhythmia.” Her gaze fixed on me. “You carry two weights. A Lament. A Prison. You seek the third. The Balance.”

She gestured to the stone on the podium. The Mechanism Stone.

“Why guard it?” Sam asked, his voice hard. “If you know what’s coming, why hide it here?”

“We are not hiding it,” Elara said, turning those bronze eyes on him. “We are studying it. This stone does not contain grief or absence. It contains *function*. The principle of ordered operation. The ritual you attempt is a mechanism. Varek understands this. You do not.”

I took a step forward. The *tock-tock-tock* of the tower was in my bones. “What does he understand?”

“That the three stones are not a lock,” she said, picking up the clear stone. The tiny gears within spun faster, casting prismatic shards across her face. “They are a tuning fork. And the frequency they are meant to calibrate is not the Echo. It is the Source. The First Memory. The wound in the world where forgetting began.” She looked from the stone to me. “Varek does not want to seal the wound. He wants to *own* it. To become the source of forgetting itself. To make every memory, every thought, a tributary that flows back to him. He doesn’t want to stop the Echo. He wants to become its architect.”

The truth of it landed in my gut, cold and heavy. Not control of the Echo. Control of the silence that made it. Control of the void where memories go. He wouldn’t just steal moments; he would own the very fabric of experience, the loom on which all stories are woven. My brother’ silence, my mother’s kiss, Kael’s fall… they would all become patterns in his tapestry.

“The ritual at the Clocktower,” I said, the words ash in my mouth. “It was a trap. For me. To use my… my healing as a vector.”

Elara nodded. “The patient is always vulnerable. The infection always arrives at the wound. You were the wound, Alex. Your reconciliation was the key he needed to turn the lock. You gave him the frequency.”

I felt the platform beneath me again, the stitches bursting. “But it failed. The balance shifted.”

“Because you loaded the scale with anchors,” she said, a flicker of something—respect?—in her metallic eyes. “You used the weight of what was truly yours. It was inelegant. Unmechanistic. It was human. It has bought you this chance.” She extended her hand, offering the Mechanism Stone. “Take it.”

I didn’t move. “Why?”

“Because the ritual must be performed correctly. At the source. Not here, in this monument to consequence, but at the origin. The Whispering Caves. Where the First Cut was made. Where the world began to forget.” She pressed the stone into my hand. It was warm, and a faint, precise vibration buzzed against my palm, syncing with the tower’s heartbeat. “We have guarded this, not to prevent the ritual, but to prevent its misuse. You are the patient. You are the wound. You must also be the suture.”

Sam stepped forward. “And Varek?”

“He will be there,” Elara said. “He has been digging at the Caves for months. His agents have hollowed out the cliffs, seeking the exact epicenter. He knows you are coming. He is preparing his own ceremony. One of ownership.” She looked at the both of us, her expression unreadable. “The mechanism is in motion. The gears are meshed. You have the three weights. Now you must set them in the correct arrangement, at the correct point, and let them find their equilibrium. If you succeed, the wound is sealed, the Echo is neutralized—a balanced, empty silence. If Varek succeeds, he becomes the new source, and all memory becomes his currency. If neither of you succeed…” She glanced upward, as if she could see through the dome to the grinding gears. “The mechanism tears itself apart. The feedback will unravel the lattice. It will not be an invasion of Echoes. It will be a great forgetting. A null-wave. It will not steal memories. It will scorch the ground where they grow.”

The quiet of the library was absolute, a bubble against the city’s roar and the tower’s tick. I held the three stones now. The blue Lament, heavy with goodbyes. The grey Prison, cold with silence. The clear Mechanism, humming with relentless order. They were weights. They were tools. They were parts of a sentence I had to speak at the one place in the world where words had first failed.

“Time?” Sam asked, the single word gritted out.

Elara moved to the wall, touched a niche. A holographic map shimmered in the air between us—a topographic display of a ragged coastline I knew. The Whispering Caves. A specific point within them glowed a sickly, angry orange. “Our readings indicate the source-point is becoming increasingly unstable. Varek’ excavations have disturbed the ancient trauma. The null-wave emission is probabilistic. We calculate you have between seventy-two and eighty hours before the probability tips over into certainty. Before the forgetting becomes autonomous.”

Three days. To get back. To find the exact point in the labyrinth of caves. To perform a ritual I didn’t fully understand, against a man who had already almost used me as a key.

I closed my fingers around the stones. Their edges bit into my flesh. The taste of my mother’s lipstick, the chemical cherry, bloomed in the back of my throat—not a sold fragment, but a flare of defiance. A memory that was still a story. Still mine.

“We’re going,” I said.

Elara simply nodded. “The door behind you leads to a freight conduit. It will take you to the eastern rail depot. Avoid the mag-lines. Varek will monitor them. Go slow. Go old.”

We turned to leave.

“Alex,” Elara called. I looked back. She stood by her podium, a priestess in a temple of ordered memory. “The ritual requires balance. Three stones. But you are the fourth weight. The variable. You are not just the wound. You are the scar. Remember what you threw into the scale. Anchors are not for holding still. They are for holding fast.”

The door hissed shut behind us, leaving us in the cool blue gloom of the conduit. The tower’s *tock-tock-tock* was fainter here, muffled by layers of steel and purpose. Sam let out a long, slow breath.

“A great forgetting,” he said, his voice hollow.

“Not if we get there first,” I said, starting down the corridor.

“And do what? You heard her. We’re walking into his prepared ground. He’s got the place dug out, probably has an army of those black-coated bastards.”

“Yes.”

“So what’s the plan?”

The conduit opened onto a metal gantry overlooking a dark shaft. A stale wind, smelling of damp and diesel, pushed up from below. The way out. I looked at the stones in my hand. The clear one’s gears spun, a miniature, perfect clock counting down.

“The plan,” I said, “is to be a variable he can’t calculate.”

We descended into the dark, leaving the measured tick of the clocktower behind. The city’s noise swelled again above us, a blanket of lies. We moved against the current, two fragments of broken memory heading for the original fracture. The anchors in my mind—the lipstick, the joke in the grass, the falling soldier—they didn’t feel like shields. They felt like hooks, set deep in the meat of who I was, tethers to keep me from dissolving into the great, mechanistic forgetting that was coming.

I had three stones. I had three days.

And I had the memory of what it cost to get them back. That was the weight I would carry into the caves. Not the hope of healing, but the cost of it. The balance wasn’t in the peace. It was in the price.

The freight conduit dumped us into a rain-slicked alley behind the rail depot. The smell of wet rust and old grease was a welcome relief from the city’s perfumed stink. Above us, the sky was a bruised purple, bleeding into the permanent electric glow. Somewhere, high in the smog, the Great Clocktower administered the hour, its face a lie of order.

Sam adjusted his cap, his eyes scanning the shadows. “East. Old roads. It’ll take time.”

“Time is the one thing we’re measuring now,” I said, pocketing the stones. Their collective hum was a quiet counter-rhythm to the city’s pulse, a reminder of a deeper, more dangerous clock.

We slipped into the stream of dockworkers and freight-handlers, becoming two more smudged faces in the gloom. The plan was formless, a direction more than a route. East. To the coast. To the wound.

But as we reached the edge of the depot, where the paved world gave way to the scarred earth of the unmaintained hinterlands, I saw it. A poster, pasted to a sagging fence, fluttering in the damp wind. It was an advertisement for a new memory-mod: “Echo-No-More! A soothing balm for intrusive recall.” The image was of a serene face, eyes closed, a single tear turning to crystal as it fell.

They were already selling the cure for a disease they didn’t know was coming. Or maybe, they did. Maybe Varek’s vision was already leaking into the world, not as an invasion, but as a market trend. The great forgetting, packaged and sold as peace.

I turned my back on it, on the city, on the clock that measured everything except what mattered. We started walking, the mud of the old road pulling at our boots. Each step was a subtraction from the countdown, a movement towards the silent, screaming place where it all began.

The hook was set. Not in the next action, but in the quiet, terrible understanding settling in my chest as we left the light behind: we weren’t just going to perform a ritual. We were going to a funeral. Either for the Echo, for Varek’s ambition, or for memory itself. And I was the one who would have to choose the eulogy.



---

# Chapter 9

## CHAPTER 9

The road east was a scar in the earth, a memory of asphalt half-digested by mud and root. We walked in the ditches when we could, keeping to the bruised shadows under the skeletal trees. The city’s glow was a fever at our backs, a false sunset that never cooled. Out here, the dark had texture. It smelled of leaf rot and stagnant water, of things returning to the soil without permission. My boots were leaden with clay. Each step was a act of archaeology, unearthing the sound of suction, the gasp of mud releasing its claim.

Sam didn’t speak. His silence was a language I’d learned. It meant listening. It meant the hum of the stones in my pocket was too loud, a beacon in the quiet. It meant we were being followed.

They didn’t come with sirens or shouts. That was for the maintained zones, for the performance of order. Out here, pursuit was a subtler thing. A snapped twig half a mile back that didn’t match the wind’s rhythm. The absence of animal sounds in a stretch of woods that should have been rustling with nightlife. A pressure, like the air before a storm, but without the promise of rain. Varek’s people. They were herding us, letting the old road do the work, letting the weight of the past pull us forward to the one place it all made sense.

“They’re there,” Sam said, finally. Not a question. A confirmation of the chill on my neck.

“I know.”

“They want us to run. To panic.”

“I know that, too.”

He glanced at me, his face a slab of shadow under the cap’s brim. “You got a third ‘I know’ for what we do about it?”

I touched the stones through the worn fabric of my coat. The black one was a cold void. The white, a thrumming, anxious heat. The grey was… stillness. A pause between heartbeats. “We keep walking to the funeral,” I said. “Guests usually follow the mourners.”

The joke fell into the mud and died. Sam’s mouth tightened, a crack in the stone of his expression. He was remembering, too. This road. The school bus with its perpetually fogged windows. The way the pines would crowd in during winter, their branches heavy with silence. We were walking back into the album of our own childhood, and someone had torn out all the good pages, leaving only the cuts where the photos had been glued down.

We walked until the dark began to dilute, not with dawn, but with a different kind of light. The sour yellow of sodium-vapor lamps, marking the outskirts of Halvern. My town. Not a hometown—that word implied a warmth, a hearth. Halvern was a place that happened to you, a collection of structures that had agreed, through sheer exhaustion, to stand near each other. The lights were a confession of weakness. They said: *Here is the edge. Beyond here, we are not responsible.*

The memory came, unbidden and unsold. The taste of the air here. Not the city’s layered stench of exhaust, fried food, and desperation. This was a flat taste. Damp concrete. Cut grass turning to seed. The faint, metallic tang from the old water tower. It was the taste of empty hours, of staring at a ceiling and knowing every crack. I had sold the big memories—the graduation, the fight with my father, the night I left. But I’d kept the textures. The boredom. The boredom was mine. It was the canvas everything else was painted on, and no one at the Emporium would give you ten credits for it.

We slipped between two failing lamp-posts, their light buzzing like trapped flies, and entered the town through its blind spot: the back of the old laundromat. The alley was a canyon of overflowing dumpsters and the sweet, rotten smell of fabric softener. From here, we could see the main street. It was wrong.

It was empty.

A Saturday night in Halvern should have had two pickup trucks circling, their radios arguing with each other. Should have had kids smoking on the bench outside the shuttered pharmacy. Should have had the flicker of television light in the windows above the hardware store. This was a curated emptiness. A staged void. The streetlights poured their jaundiced light onto nothing. It was a memory of a street, not the street itself.

“Hostages,” Sam breathed the word, and it misted in the cold air. He wasn’t guessing. He was reading the scene. The absence was the message. *We have them. They are elsewhere. Come and see.*

The caves were at the north end of town, past the scrap of woods we called the Gully. To get there, we’d have to cross the open, corpse-gray expanse of the high school football field. A killing floor.

“We go around,” I said. “Through the creek bed.”

Sam nodded. It was longer. It was wet. It was the route of small, scared animals. It was ours.

We moved like water, downhill, into the ditch where Miller’s Creek pretended to be a river every spring. It was a trickle now, a thread of black water stitching through the stones. The banks were high enough to hide us. The sound of our passage was lost in the water’s quiet gossip. Above the banks, the world was a strip of ominous sky.

We were halfway when the first shot rang out.

It wasn’t the sharp crack of city gunfire. It was a deeper, older sound—a *thump* that vibrated in the chest, followed by the whine of something tearing the air a foot above our heads. A slug from a industrial rivet gun, repurposed. A dockworker’s weapon. They weren’t just herding anymore.

“Go!” Sam shoved me forward, turning, his own piece—a compact coil-gun—already in his hand. He returned fire not at the source, but at the lamp-post at the edge of the field. The glass shattered and died, plunging a section of the bank into deeper shadow. A professional’s move. Create confusion, not carnage.

I scrambled forward, the creek bed’s stones rolling under me. Another shot. This one sparked off a rock to my left, showering my cheek with stinging fragments. I didn’t stop. The mouth of the Gully was ahead, a darker maw in the night. The trees would be cover. The trees remembered me.

I plunged into the woods, the familiar, skeletal arms of the oaks catching at my coat. I didn’t look back for Sam. He was either behind me or he was buying time. Either way, stopping was a betrayal. The map of this place was etched in a part of my brain that had never been for sale. The gnarled root that tripped everyone. The fallen log that was hollow and full of spiders. The path that seemed clear but led to the thorn-choked ravine. I took the spider-log path, the one only kids and fugitives used.

I could hear them behind me, crashing through the underbrush. More than two. Their voices were low, coordinated. They weren’t shouting. They were communicating in clipped, efficient codes. Varek’s private choir. I pushed harder, my lungs burning with the cold air and the ghost of childhood asthma.

Then the trees opened up, and there it was.

The bluff face, pale and pocked like old bone in the gloom. The cave mouth was a dark, vertical slit, a crack in the world. And arrayed before it, on their knees in the damp leaves, were the people of Halvern.

Maybe thirty of them. Old Man Barrett from the hardware store. Mrs. Finley, who used to substitute teach. The Garrity kids, their faces smudged with tears and dirt. They were silent, their mouths sealed with thick, grey strips of industrial adhesive tape. Their hands were bound behind them with plastic ties. Behind each stood a figure in dark, unmarked tactical gear, a hand on each hostage’s shoulder. They were statues in a tableau of quiet terror.

And in the center, standing before the cave mouth as if it were his own front door, was Varek.

He wore a long, dark coat, open to reveal a simple grey tunic beneath. He looked like a scholar who had wandered into a war zone by accident. His hands were clasped loosely behind his back. His face was illuminated by a single portable work-light set on the ground, carving his features into planes of light and shadow. He looked… patient.

“Alex,” he called, his voice carrying in the cold air without needing to shout. It was the tone you’d use to call someone in for dinner. “You made good time. The road remembers its own, I suppose.”

I stepped out of the tree line, my hands open at my sides. The stones were a furnace and an icebox in my pocket. “Let them go, Varek. This is between us and the hole in the world.”

“Is it?” He tilted his head. “I disagree. This is about community. About sacrifice. They are the community.” He gestured to the kneeling figures. “And you are the sacrifice. Or rather, your connection is. The final thread that needs to be snipped so the new tapestry can be woven.” He took a step forward. “Give me the stones, Alex. Perform the severance ritual with me. Not to close the wound, but to *direct* it. To let it wash over this place, this starting point, and then… let it flow outward. A gentle tide of amnesia. No more pain of what was. Only the peace of what is.”

Mrs. Finley was looking at me. Her eyes, above the grey tape, were wide with a fear that had moved past panic into a kind of hollow recognition. She looked at me like I was a ghost. The Garrity kid, the younger one, was shaking.

“You’re selling the tide,” I said, the pieces clicking into place with a cold, final logic. “Echo-No-More. You’re not trying to stop the Echo. You’re *branding* it. You’ll be the one who controls the faucet of forgetting. The ultimate monopoly. Not on memory, but on its absence.”

A faint, approving smile touched his lips. “You always were quick. It’s not destruction. It’s… curation. We keep the useful memories—skills, language, obedience. We let the messy, painful, *individual* ones drain away. No more trauma. No more rebellion born of remembered wrongs. A clean, quiet world.”

“A dead world.”
“A *peaceful* one.”
“Where you hold the keys to the past.”
“Someone must.” His smile faded. “The stones, Alex. Or I give the order. We start with the children. A different kind of sacrifice, but the point will be made.”

The man behind the younger Garrity kid tightened his grip, a knife appearing in his other hand. The metal caught the work-light’s gleam.

My blood was thunder in my ears. The stones seemed to pulse in time with it. The black one whispered of an end to all this noise. The white one screamed to fight, to burn, to remember every slight and use it as fuel. The grey one just… waited.

I was reaching for my pocket, my fingers numb, when the diversion came.

It didn’t come from the woods. It came from the cave.

A figure stumbled out of the black slit, clutching the rock wall for support. He was gaunt, clothes torn and stained with something dark, one arm held tight against his ribs. His face was a mask of pain and grim purpose, but the eyes were unmistakable.

Kael.

A ripple went through Varek’s men. They hadn’t posted a guard inside. Why would they? The cave was the threat, the wound. No one would come *out* of it.

Kael’s voice was a ragged scrape, but it carried. “The chamber! It’s destabilizing! The feedback loop—it’ll pull the whole bluff down!”

It was a lie. A beautiful, desperate lie. But he sold it with the conviction of a dying man. He even pointed a trembling finger back into the darkness, as if at some cataclysm only he could see.

Varek’s head snapped toward him, his cool composure fracturing for one second. “Impossible. The resonance is contained—”

That second was all Sam needed.

A shot, from the tree line to the far left. Not at Varek. At the work-light. It exploded in a shower of glass and sparks, plunging the clearing into near-darkness.

Chaosis a language everyone understands. The hostages screamed against their gags, rolling, scrambling. The guards, trained for control, were blinded for a critical moment. Shouts erupted. Muzzle flashes lit the scene in stuttering, hellish snapshots.

“Alex! Go!” Kael’s voice, raw and urgent.

I didn’t think. I ran. Not away from the cave, but into it. I passed within feet of Varek, who was shouting orders, trying to reassemble his control. Our eyes met in a strobe of gunfire. His were wide with a fury that was almost childlike in its disappointment. Mine, I’m sure, were just empty. I was a vessel, running on a script written by stones and ghosts.

I plunged into the mouth of the cave, and the world changed.

The temperature dropped. The sound of the chaos outside became muffled, then distant, as if heard through a wall of thick felt. But it was replaced by another sound. A sound that was inside my skull.

It was a hum, but lower than any soundwave. It was a pressure. A memory of a sound. The Echo.

The path was familiar, a downward slope worn smooth by generations of kids daring each other to go deeper. I didn’t need a light. The stones in my pocket were providing their own. A sickly, phosphorescent glow leaked from the fabric, painting the wet walls in pulses of anti-light. The black stone drank the shadows. The white one fought them. The grey one translated.

Deeper. Past the first chamber where we’d had our first, fumbling drink of stolen beer. Past the narrow squeeze called the “Birthing Crack.” The air grew thicker, tasting of wet stone and something else. Ozone. And beneath that, the copper-tang of blood that wasn’t blood—it was the scent of exposed time.

I reached the final chamber. The heart.

It was smaller than I remembered. The ceiling was low, dripping. In the center, the floor wasn’t stone. It was a pool of… not water. It had a viscosity, a sheen. It was a mirror, but one that reflected nothing in the room. It reflected other places. Other times. Flickers: a child’s birthday party (mine?). A car spinning on ice (not mine). A woman’s hands knitting (my grandmother’s?). A million fragments, bleeding together in the silvery, mercurial surface. This was the wound. Not a hole in the rock, but a hole in the layer cake of reality. This was where Varek, years ago, had tried to pry open a seam and peek at what was underneath. He hadn’t created the Echo. He’d just poked a sleeping tumor.

And the ritual to close it was simple. It was written in the resonance of the stones. The knowledge unspooled in my mind, cold and clear. I had to stand at the edge. I had to hold the three stones—the anchor, the catalyst, the keystone—and I had to remember. Not a specific memory. I had to remember *myself*. The whole, continuous, messy thread of Alex. From the taste of lipstick to the mud on my boots. That thread, fed into the wound, would act as a suture. A self to seal the lack of self.

But Varek’s ritual was the opposite. He would use the stones to cut my thread. To use my unraveling as a filament to guide the Echo outward, a wick for the void.

Footsteps, careful and swift, echoed down the passage behind me.

He’d left his men to handle the chaos. He’d come for the prize.

Varek entered the chamber. He was slightly breathless, a lock of hair fallen across his forehead. It made him look human. More dangerous. He saw me standing at the pool’s edge, the stones now in my cupped hands, glowing with their tripartite light.

“Don’t be a monument, Alex,” he said, his voice soft, almost tender in the cavern’s hush. “Monuments are for dead things. Be a river. Change course.”

“This isn’t a course,” I said, my voice sounding strange to me. Hollowed out by the hum. “It’s a cancer. You don’t guide it. You cut it out.”

“You can’t cut out time.” He took a step closer. “Think of it. No more pain from this place. No more memory of your mother’s empty chair. Your father’s disappointed silence. The crushing smallness of these walls. I’m offering you freedom.”

He was good. He’d read my file, the memories I’d sold. He was painting a paradise of emptiness. And a part of me, the tired, nineteen-year-old in the nail-salon chair, wanted to take it. To let go.

I looked down at the stones. The black one promised an end to longing. The white one promised an end to his lies. The grey one promised nothing. It just was.

I made my choice.

I didn’t throw the stones into the pool. I didn’t recite words. I simply held them tight, closed my eyes, and did the one thing I’d built a life around not doing: I remembered *deliberately*.

Not the curated highlights. The garbage data. The boredom of Halvern. The sting of the schoolyard gravel. The smell of my own fear in this very cave on a dare. The feel of the sixty credits in my hand after selling the lipstick. The hollow victory of it. Sam’s silence, which meant trust. Kael’s sacrifice, which meant a debt I could never repay. The taste of the city’s air. The weight of the stones. The sound of Mrs. Finley’s screaming eyes.

I fed it all into the stones. I was the thread.

The reaction was instantaneous.

The glowing pool erupted. Not with water, but with images. A silent, screaming torrent of memories—mine, others’, fragments of the town, of people I’d never met—geysered upward, filling the chamber with a storm of silent cinema. A carousel of pain and joy and mundane moments, spinning, merging, dissolving.

Varek cried out, but not in fear. In rage. “No! You’re wasting it! You’re burying it!”

He lunged for me, not for the stones, but for *me*, to break my concentration, to sever the thread by force.

His hands closed on my shoulders. And the moment he touched me, he was in the circuit.

His memories flooded into the stream.

I saw him as a young man, brilliant and hungry, standing in this chamber with crude instruments. I saw his ambition, clean and sharp as a scalpel. I saw the moment the rock had *sighed* open, and the silent, howling void of forgotten time had brushed his mind. I saw it not as a trauma, but as a revelation. The ultimate blank slate. And I saw his plan, not as monstrous, but as beautifully, logically serene. A world without the screaming in its head. A cure for the human condition.

He saw me. He saw the lipstick memory. He saw the shame of the sale. He saw my love for Sam and Kael, a clumsy, fragile thing I’d never named. He saw my small, stubborn refusal to let go, even of the pain, because it was *mine*.

We were not fighting with fists or weapons. We were fighting with contexts. His grand, serene, universal cure. My small, selfish, messy claim to a single, continuous life.

The stones were the arbiter. The black one drank at his vision of peace. The white one burned with my defiance. The grey one held us both, suspended in the moment of conflict.

The pool was reacting. The geyser of memories began to coil, to twist in on itself. It was forming a shape. A knot. A seal. My thread was stitching the wound closed, but his vision was tangled in it, a counter-thread trying to redirect the pattern.

The pressure built. It was in my teeth, in the marrow of my bones. I was being unmade. Not erased, but… stretched. Across the gap. I was becoming the suture. I could feel my earliest memories—the lipstick, the feel of my childhood blanket—fraying, thinning, as they were woven into the fabric of the sealing wound.

Varek screamed. It was a sound of pure loss. He wasn’t being erased. He was being *understood*. And in my memory-stream, he saw his own theory reflected back at him: that to be known completely is to be defanged. His grand cure was being reduced to a boy’s trauma in a cave, a mind that couldn’t bear the noise of the world and so decided to silence it for everyone.

“Stop!” he begged. Not a command. A plea.

I couldn’t stop. The ritual had its own momentum now. The stones were using us as conductors. The grey stone, the keystone, began to grow warm, then hot. It was making the choice. The balance.

With a sound like a universe sighing, the glowing pool imploded.

Not an explosion inward, but a *collapse*. The memory-storm sucked back into the silvery surface, which hardened, darkened, became mere, mundane wet stone. The hum ceased. The pressure vanished.

The silence was absolute. It was the silence after a great noise, ringing and profound.

Varek reeled back, releasing me. He fell to his knees, staring at the now-dark floor. His face was ashen. He looked… ordinary. Confused. The certainty was gone from his eyes, replaced by a hollow bewilderment.

I was still standing. But I felt thin. Translucent. I looked at my hands. They were solid. But inside… I had gaps. I knew I had a memory of my seventh birthday. I could feel the shape of it, the emotional silhouette: cake, a feeling of warmth. But the details—the color of the frosting, the song they sang—were gone. They were part of the suture. The price of the thread.

I had paid in memory. Not with a clean extraction, but with a ragged tax. I had sealed the wound with pieces of myself.

The stones in my hands were inert. Just rocks. Their light was gone. Their hum was silent. The job was done.

I pocketed them. They were mine now, truly mine. Receipts for a purchase no one should ever have to make.

I turned and walked back up the passage, past Varek, who didn’t move. He was just kneeling in the dark, touching the ordinary stone, weeping quietly for a paradise he could no longer quite remember.

When I emerged into the night, the clearing was a different kind of chaos. Sam was there, a cut on his forehead leaking a dark line down his temple. He was helping Kael sit against a tree. The hostages were free, their tape and ties cut, huddled together, being tended to by a few of Varek’s men who seemed to have lost their purpose, their weapons lowered. The fight had gone out of everyone. The wound was closed. The urgency had bled away.

Sam saw me. He read everything in my walk, in my face. He didn’t ask if we’d won. He just nodded, once.

I walked to them. Kael looked up, his face pale with pain and exhaustion. “Did it…?”

“It’s closed,” I said, my voice rough. “He’s still in there. But he’s… not a problem anymore.”

Kael closed his eyes, a long breath escaping him.

I looked east, past the bluff. Towards the coast. The wound there, the original, the mother of all Echoes… it was still open. This had just been a suture on a single, leaking vein. A test.

And I had paid for it. I could feel the absences in me, like missing teeth my tongue kept finding. I was less than I was an hour ago. I was a book with paragraphs carefully scissored out, the story still holding, but forever jarring at the gaps.

Sam followed my gaze. “We stopped one.”
“We did,” I said.
“The big one’s still out there.”
“I know.”

He was quiet for a moment, watching the freed townsfolk begin to murmur, to point at the cave, to look at me with a new, unreadable fear. “What did it cost you, Alex?”

I thought of the missing birthday, the other gaps I hadn’t yet discovered. I thought of the stones, heavy and silent in my pocket. I thought of Varek, kneeling in the dark, crying for a dream he could no longer describe.

“I’m not sure yet,” I said. And that was the truth. The final, quiet terror of it wasn’t in the fight, or the sacrifice. It was in the walking away, knowing you’d left pieces of yourself behind as mortar, and that every step forward from here would be on a road made just a little more hollow. The hook was set, deeper now, in the very fabric of what I was: we had averted a market trend for oblivion. But the source remained. And the next time, the currency they’d demand might not be memory.

It might be me.



---

# Chapter 10

## CHAPTER 10

The ritual wasn’t in the stones. It was in the silence between them.

I knelt in the mouth of the cave, the three river-smooth weights cold against my palm. Sam and Kael were a ragged barricade ten yards behind me, their backs to my work. I heard the scuff of boots on shale, the low, threatening murmur of Varek’s men as they fanned out. Sam’s knuckles were white on his crowbar. Kael held a length of rusted pipe, his breath coming in sharp, pained hitches from the beating he’d taken. They were a still life of futile courage. I had to make it count.

The instructions weren’t instructions. They were a feeling. A pattern of absence. The old woman at the ferry had pressed the stones into my hand and in that touch was a memory that wasn’t mine: the deep, resonant quiet of a forest after a heavy snow, a silence so complete it became a kind of sound. That was the seal. Not a shout, but a hush.

I closed my eyes. The world didn’t disappear. It inverted. The physical bled away—the chill of the cave air, the grit under my knees, the metallic taste of fear—and the underlying architecture of the place revealed itself. It was like seeing the scars on a healed wound. The Echo wasn’t a thing in the cave; the cave was a thin spot in the thing. A blister on the skin of the world, weeping a slow, psychic pus. The energy here was a low, hungry thrum, a vibration that wanted to match the frequency of forgetting.

My own memories were a jangling, discordant chorus in comparison. The taste of the lipstick. The smell of the Emporium’s acetone. Sam’s laugh on a good day. They were bright, specific, and painfully mortal. They were the wrong kind of quiet.

I had to find a deeper one.

I reached for the memory of my mother’s lipstick, not to sell it, but to *place* it. To use it as a landmark. I held the waxy cherry taste on my tongue, the press of her kiss on my forehead, and I set it down in the dark of my mind like a single lit candle. Then I pushed it away. I reached for the memory before that. Before the memory. The blank space that comes before the first thing you know.

It was like trying to recall the shape of a room after the lights go out. You know the walls are there because you can’t move. That was the silence I needed. The silence before the first memory formed. The silence of being, not of knowing.

I poured that not-memory into the stones.

They grew warm. Not with heat, but with a profound, absorbing neutrality. A spiritual blotters. The thrumming in the cave dimmed, dampened. The air grew heavier, thicker, like the moment before a thunderstorm breaks.

“He’s coming,” Sam said, his voice tight.

I didn’t open my eyes. The ritual was a thread I had to hold, a note I had to sustain. To look away was to snap it.

Boots crunched, deliberate and close. I felt Sam and Kael shift, their shadows falling over me. A standoff.

“The scribbler,” a voice said. It was Varek, but it was wrong. It had a texture to it, like gravel stirred in honey. It was layered, as if multiple people were speaking just out of sync. “Kneeling in the dirt. How poetic.”

I kept my breath even, my focus on the stones, on the spreading silence. It was seeping into the edges of the blister, a clear resin filling a crack.

“You think you’re closing a door,” Varek said. He was circling. I could feel his gaze like a physical pressure. “You’re just choosing which side to be on when it locks. They’ll never understand what you did here. They’ll look at you and see the problem, not the solution. Fear is the only language they have for power they can’t catalog.”

The thrumming fought back. It pulsed, a wave of dizzying negation. It offered a simpler path: to just let go. To let the quiet become an emptiness. It was seductive. My shoulders ached. The memory-landmark of my mother’s kiss flickered.

“Hold the line,” Kael grunted, and there was a scuffle, a cry of pain that wasn’t his.

“Let him work,” Sam snarled.

I couldn’t. The thread was slipping. The ritual required a sacrifice of noise, of self. I was too full of both. I was all sharp edges and recent wounds, a cacophony of fear and stubbornness. The silence wouldn’t take.

Varek laughed, a sound that crackled like static. “You see? You’re trying to give it a gift it doesn’t want. Your precious, fragile little memories. It doesn’t want souvenirs. It wants an eraser.”

His shadow fell over me completely. I opened my eyes.

He stood between me and the cave’s dark heart. He was both more and less than the man I’d met in the velvet-draped room. His fine clothes were smudged with dirt and something dark that wasn’t blood. His eyes were the worst part. They weren’t voids. They were *full*. A frantic, swirling collage of stolen moments—a child’s birthday gasp, a last breath, the split-second before a crash. He was a walking, talking scrapbook of other people’s finales.

In his hand, he held not a weapon, but a fragment. It looked like a shard of obsidian, but it swam with an internal, greyish light. A captured piece of the Echo itself, raw and unformed. A seed of the great quiet.

“You trade in fragments,” he said, his layered voice almost gentle. “I’m offering you the source. The blank page. Not just to sell, Alex. To *write on*. With this… with what’s in that cave… you don’t have to be the one who loses pieces. You can be the one who decides what pieces everyone else gets to keep.”

He extended the shard. The grey light pulsed in time with the cave’s thrum. It whispered without words. It showed me, not in images but in pure, emotional concept, what he meant. I wouldn’t be a memory thief. I’d be a memory architect. I could take the raw clay of human experience and sculpt it. No more pain. No more regret. I could smooth the rough edges of the world. I could give people the pasts they deserved, not the ones they’d endured. The power was dizzying. It was a clean, clinical godhood.

It was the Memory Emporium on a cosmic scale.

I saw myself in a room that wasn’t a converted nail salon. It was pristine, white. People wouldn’t come in desperate. They’d come in grateful. I wouldn’t take sixty credits for a mother’s kiss. I’d give them a better one. A perfect one. The hook of shame, the hollow after the sale… it would be gone. Replaced by the warm glow of curation.

All I had to do was stop pouring my quiet into the stones. All I had to do was reach out and take his.
